    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e33e5bd2273a4a14b72c8e356e1b3d30">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '08fc47d9-f387-4796-8716-cc7f0fe03c2e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d28003480" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as a promising alternative for next-generation energy storage solutions, driven by the economic and environmental benefits of abundant sodium resources. A pivotal aspect of SIB advancement is the development of advanced electrolytes, which remains a formidable challenge. Herein, a facile and scalable synthesis method for low-cost sodium-difluoro(oxalato)borate (NaDFOB) is reported and explored its application as a standalone electrolyte salt for SIBs. The NaDFOB-based ether electrolyte demonstrates exceptional electrochemical stability, solvent compatibility, and the unique capacity to form a dense, robust solid-electrolyte interphase layer on electrode surfaces. As a result, the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) cathode with NaDFOB-based electrolyte exhibits ultrahigh cycling stability with a remarkable capacity retention of 98.7% after 1000 cycles. Furthermore, an Ah-level hard carbon (HC)//NFPP pouch cell using NaDFOB-based ether electrolyte shows an impressive cycle life of 500 cycles, coupled with an average Coulombic efficiency of 99.9%. The pouch cells also maintain superior electrochemical performance across a broad temperature range from −40 to 60&nbsp;°C, showcasing the electrolyte's versatility. This work contributes significant insights into the strategic design and application of innovative salts, paving the way for longer-lasting SIBs with enhanced electrochemical performance.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403306-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403306-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been an attractive alternative for the post lithium-ion battery due to the wide abundance and low cost of sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0001_R_d28003387e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0007_R_d28003387e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Environ. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrolyte is known as the “blood” of SIBs and interacts closely with the cathode and anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0008_R_d28003387e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Today Chem.">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0013_R_d28003387e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Trans. Tianjin Univ.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrolyte not only plays an important role in regulating the properties of the electrode/electrolyte interface but also has a crucial impact on the battery performance, including reversible capacity, rate capability, cycling stability, high and low-temperature performance, and safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0014_R_d28003387e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>-<a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0020_R_d28003387e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is very important to explore advanced electrolytes to achieve high-performance SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The sodium salt is the main component of electrolytes of SIBs, which directly determines the electrochemical characteristics of electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0021_R_d28003387e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Mater. Adv.">21</a>-<a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0023_R_d28003387e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At present, the commonly used sodium salts are mainly sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>), sodium perchlorate (NaClO<sub>4</sub>), sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI), and sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0024_R_d28003387e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Environ. Sci.">24</a>-<a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0027_R_d28003387e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Commun.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, all these salts have shortcomings for large-scale application. For example, NaClO<sub>4</sub> is very explosive and its adsorbed H<sub>2</sub>O is too difficult to dry out.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0028_R_d28003387e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> is toxic and expensive, easy to decompose at elevated temperatures and under humidity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0005_R_d28003387e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0014_R_d28003387e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NaTFSI and NaFSI are of high cost, and the TFSI<sup>−</sup> (or FSI<sup>−</sup>) anion in NaTFSI (or NaFSI) has a corrosive effect on the aluminum foil collector.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0008_R_d28003387e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Today Chem.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, it is of great significance to explore advanced salts to promote the wide applicability of SIBs. Sodium difluoro(oxalato)borate (NaDFOB), which has non-localized electrons in the structure and weak bonds between sodium-ions and anions, has shown fascinating properties, such as high conductivity, compatibility with a variety of solvents, and own film-forming characteristics with no need for film-forming additives.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0014_R_d28003387e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0029_R_d28003387e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Commun.">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0030_R_d28003387e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the use of NaDFOB as a single salt for electrolytes and their compatibility with different SIB electrodes have rarely been reported. One reason may be the concern of its high cost as the current synthesis process for NaDFOB is complex, dangerous, and costly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0029_R_d28003387e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this study, we introduce a novel, cost-effective synthesis method for NaDFOB using a liquid precipitation technique. We have thoroughly examined its application as a single salt for the electrolytes of SIBs. The NaDFOB facilitates the formation of a dense and robust cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) layer on the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) cathode, significantly enhances its cyclability and Coulombic efficiency (CE). Specifically, the NFPP electrode demonstrates an impressive capacity retention of 98.7% after 1000 cycles along with an average CE of 99.96%. Moreover, the hard carbon (HC)//NFPP pouch cell with NaDFOB-diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (G2) electrolyte shows an extended cycling lifespan of 500 cycles and commendable low-temperature performance. Our findings contribute to the exploration of affordable and functional sodium salts, thereby advancing the practical implementation of SIBs in various applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403306-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403306-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>NaDFOB was synthesized through a facile precipitation method (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0001">1a</a>). Oxalic acid (H<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) and boric acid (H<sub>3</sub>BO<sub>3</sub>) were dissolved in H<sub>2</sub>O, then sodium fluoride (NaF) was added and the solution was hydrothermally treated at 160&nbsp;°C for 7&nbsp;h. It should be noted that the method does not involve the direct use of hydrogen fluoride (HF) and can be easily operated in a laboratory environment without specialized equipment. Whereas the current synthesis process for NaDFOB typically relies on boron-trifluoride-diethyl-etherate (BF<sub>3</sub> ether) or HF as the starting materials. These substances are not only toxic and highly corrosive, making them tricky to handle, but they are also quite expensive.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0029_R_d28003387e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern shows that the synthesized NaDFOB salt has high crystallinity and the XRD pattern is well consistent with the reported work, indicating the successful synthesis of the NaDFOB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0001">1b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0029_R_d28003387e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A liquid nuclear magnetic (NMR) resonance spectrometer was used to evaluate the purity of salt because it can identify all soluble chemicals, even if they exist at a low concentration (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0001">1c</a>). The strong peaks at 2.93&nbsp;ppm and −150.71&nbsp;ppm in <sup>11</sup>B NMR and <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra confirm the formation of DFOB<sup>−</sup> anion, while the <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectrum is consistent with the carbon in NaDFOB.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0008_R_d28003387e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Today Chem.">8</a>, <a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0029_R_d28003387e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All these results indicate the successful synthesis of the NaDFOB with high purity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403306-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d3709a27-d631-4325-8ab2-da09db693192/aenm202403306-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d3709a27-d631-4325-8ab2-da09db693192/aenm202403306-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/02bdfacf-9796-49f8-aa1f-ba8b7471aec6/aenm202403306-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d3709a27-d631-4325-8ab2-da09db693192/aenm202403306-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403306-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403306" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The schematic illustration of the synthesis of NaDFOB from H<sub>3</sub>BO<sub>3</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4,</sub> and NaF. b) The XRD pattern and c) <sup>11</sup>B, <sup>13</sup>C, and <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra of as-synthesized NaDFOB. e) Ionic conductivity of NaDFOB dissolved in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (G2) at different concentrations. d) The conductivity versus temperature curve and f) LSV curve of the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaDFOB-G2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrolytes with different concentrations of NaDFOB in G2 were prepared, which allows for concentrations greater than 2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). According to the conductivity-concentration curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0001">1d</a>), the conductivity of the electrolytes increases with increasing concentration. Impressively, the conductivity of the electrolyte reaches up to 6.8 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> and achieves the highest value of 7.94 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at 1.4&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>. To make a better contrast with conventional electrolytes, the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte was chosen for subsequent characterization. The ionic conductivity of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte at different temperatures was tested. With the decrease in temperature, the ionic conductivity of the electrolyte gradually reduces (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0001">1e</a>). Notably, the ionic conductivity of NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte still maintains 0.99 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C, much higher than that of the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2 (0.27 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The oxidation potential of the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte was measured by the linear sweep voltammetry (LSV). It shows that the electrolyte has a high oxidation potential of &gt;5.2&nbsp;V, indicating its potential compatibility with most cathode materials.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical compatibility of the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte with the NFPP cathode was investigated by assembling Na//NFPP half-cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the cycle voltammetry (CV) curves of the NFPP electrode in the first three cycles using the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte. The NFPP electrode shows a pair of dominant redox peaks at 3.06/3.45&nbsp;V with weak redox peaks at 2.62/3.27&nbsp;V, which correspond to the insertion/extraction process of Na ions at different sites. Two typical voltage plateaus at 3.09/3.18&nbsp;V and 2.66/3.21&nbsp;V are observed for charge/discharge curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0002">2b</a>), which is in good accordance with the two main redox peaks in CV curves. The reduction peak observed at 2.5&nbsp;V is likely due to the reduction reaction of DFOB<sup>−</sup> ions on the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0029_R_d28003387e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Commun.">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0031_R_d28003387e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a>-<a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0034_R_d28003387e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Energy">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Impressively, the NFPP cathode showed high reversibility in the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte, exhibiting an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 93% with overlapped charge/discharge curves even after 1000 cycles. The capacity retention rate is 98.7% after 1000 cycles and the average CE is as high as 99.96% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0002">2c</a>). To further understand the influence of NaDFOB-G2 electrolytes on the properties of the electrode surface, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of cells during cycling was tested. In order to eliminate the irrationality of impedance fitting caused by subjective factors, the distribution of relaxation time (DRT)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0035_R_d28003387e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Joule">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0036_R_d28003387e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Environ. Sci.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was introduced to fit the impedance data to provide an objective basis for peak separation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0002">2e</a>). Therefore, specific frequencies of different interfaces corresponding to the impedance of each cell can be obtained by fitting. It was found that the values of impedance of CEI (R<sub>CEI</sub>) and charge transfer impedance (R<sub>ct</sub>) in the battery decreased during cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The values of R<sub>CEI</sub>+R<sub>ct</sub> in the 1st, 10th, 20th, and 50th cycles are 2567, 1644, 1117, and 979 Ω, respectively. This means that the CEI will be stabilized after a long cycle, which guarantees the stable cycling of the cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0031_R_d28003387e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, when charge/discharge at current rates of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 4, 5, and 8 C, the reversible capacities of NFPP electrode are 91.7, 90.5, 89.5, 88.2, 86.8, 86.1 and 84.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a high current rate of 10 C, a high reversible capacity of 83.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved. When the current density returns to 0.2 C, the initial capacity of the electrode can be almost completely restored, indicating that the NFPP electrode has good reversibility (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0002">2f</a>). All these results indicate that the NFPP electrodes show comparable electrochemical performance in NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte than in NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2 electrolyte in terms of reversibility, long-term stability, and rate performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). To show the potential for low-temperature application, the NFPP electrodes with different electrolytes were tested at low temperatures. Notably, the NFPP electrode with NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte exhibits a capacity retention of 92.7% at −20&nbsp;°C compared to that at 25&nbsp;°C. Even at −40&nbsp;°C, a high capacity retention of 84.8% is achieved (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0002">2g,h</a>). Impressively, the NFPP electrode with the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte retains higher capacity retention than that with NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2 electrolyte at both −20 and −40&nbsp;°C (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte works well with other cathode materials. Both the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> electrodes show high cycling stability over 200 cycles (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the compatibility of the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte with the hard carbon (HC) anode is also evaluated. The HC electrode has a large overpotential at room temperature and shows a small capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), which may be result of the thick SEI film on the Na metal surface slowing down the speed of sodium-ions passing through the SEI film and increase the polarization of the cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0008_R_d28003387e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Today Chem.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403306-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/97a197ad-856c-4ec7-8f7e-6ce46fa70200/aenm202403306-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/97a197ad-856c-4ec7-8f7e-6ce46fa70200/aenm202403306-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9762f571-b17a-4ed1-b3a2-5131a6fb1b7e/aenm202403306-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/97a197ad-856c-4ec7-8f7e-6ce46fa70200/aenm202403306-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403306-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403306" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycle voltammetry curves of NFPP electrode in the first three cycles, measured at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 1.5 and 4&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. b) Charge/discharge curves of the NFPP electrode at the 10th, 50th, 500th, and 1000th cycles. c) Cycling performance of the NFPP electrode at 2 C. d) EIS Nyquist plots of NFPP electrode at the 1st, 10th, 20th, and 50th cycles. e) The corresponding distributions of relaxation times are evaluated by the DRT. The characteristic peak corresponding to R<sub>Diffusion</sub> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0002">2e</a> is only automatically fitted by the software, and no corresponding frequency exists in the actual data. f) Rate performance of the NFPP electrode. g) Discharge curves and h) capacities of the NFPP electrode at 25, −20, and −40&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To avoid the influence of the high polarization from Na metal, HC//NFPP full coin-cells were assembled. The HC//NFPP full cell shows stable cycling performance over 100 cycles with a high average CE (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the high combability of the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte in the full cells. An Ah-level HC//NFPP pouch cells were further assembled to test the feasibility of the practical application of the NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte. The HC//NFPP pouch cell delivers a reversible capacity of 1.029 Ah and ICE of 82.5% (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0003">3a</a>). Meanwhile, the pouch cell exhibits excellent cycling stability with no swelling during the cycling process. Specifically, the pouch cell manifests a high capacity retention of 80% after 500 cycles at a current rate of 1 C, along with a high average CE of 99.9% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0003">3b</a>). Moreover, the pouch cell shows stable performance at different temperatures. The discharge capacities are 0.985, 0.884, and 1.06 Ah at −20, −40, and 60&nbsp;°C (0.1 C), corresponding to 95.7%, 85.9%, and 103% of the capacity retention, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0003">3c</a>). Even at a higher discharge rate of 0.5 C at 60&nbsp;°C, the battery can still maintain 103% of its capacity at 25&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The HC//NFPP pouch cell shows outstanding cycling stability at −20 and 60&nbsp;°C with high capacity retention over hundreds of cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0003">3d,e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). These results indicated the high combability of NaDFOB-G2 electrolytes for practical pouch cells.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403306-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ecb7fc22-30a1-4e93-b59c-270e89fb169d/aenm202403306-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ecb7fc22-30a1-4e93-b59c-270e89fb169d/aenm202403306-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e5583d46-18a3-4d69-844e-d13835ad3222/aenm202403306-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ecb7fc22-30a1-4e93-b59c-270e89fb169d/aenm202403306-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403306-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403306" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Initial charge–discharge curve of HC//NFPP pouch cell using NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte at a rate of 0.1 C (1 C = 1000&nbsp;mA). b) Cycling performance of HC//NFPP pouch cell at 1 C rate. c) Discharge curves of the HC//NFPP pouch cell at −20, −40, and 60&nbsp;°C, respectively. d) Cycling performance of the HC//NFPP pouch at (d) low temperature (−20&nbsp;°C, 0.2 C) and e) high temperature (60&nbsp;°C, 1 C).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To reveal the underlying reason for the good electrochemical performance of the full cells. The NFPP and HC electrodes in the cycled full cell were taken out for characterization. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images show that a uniform layer of SEI film was formed on the surface of both cathode and anode electrode materials (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0004">4a,e</a>). This is beneficial to the long-term cycle stability of the battery. Time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) were conducted to unravel the components and structure of the SEI films.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0037_R_d28003387e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Environ. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NFPP and HC electrodes contain a large number of B and F elements in the longitudinal space at a certain depth (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0004">4b–d,f,g</a>). This is due to the redox decomposition of DFOB<sup>−</sup> to promote the formation of fluoride and boron-containing compounds in SEI film well, resulting in improved structural and electrochemical stability. XPS was further employed to understand the composition of SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403306-fig-0004">4i,j</a>). In the C 1s spectra, the signals of C─C (284.5&nbsp;eV), C─OR (286.5&nbsp;eV), C═O (288.5&nbsp;eV), and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (289.6&nbsp;eV) can be detected. Organic components C═O and C─OR may come from the reduction of G2 solvents, while Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> may be from the decomposition of DFOB<sup>−</sup> anions. The wide signal of O 1s spectra can be deconvolved into C─O (532.1&nbsp;eV) and B─O (532.6&nbsp;eV), and three signals corresponding to B─F, B<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3,</sub> and Na─B─O can be observed in the B 1s spectra. F 1s spectra confirm the existence of NaF in the surface layer. These inorganic compounds containing B and F are mainly produced by the decomposition of DFOB<sup>−</sup>-anions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0030_R_d28003387e872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a>, <a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0038_R_d28003387e872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Sci. Adv.">38</a>, <a href="#aenm202403306-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403306-bib-0039_R_d28003387e872" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on XPS results, the chemical composition of DFOB<sup>−</sup>-derived SEI of cathode and anode electrodes is the same. Therefore, NaDFOB plays a vital role in forming robust SEI on the cathode and anode electrodes in G2-based electrolytes, resulting in good compatibility with different cathode and anode materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403306-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2d9838f7-cd86-456c-913e-0a1a33df0af7/aenm202403306-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2d9838f7-cd86-456c-913e-0a1a33df0af7/aenm202403306-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/296c1c8c-7a02-4d06-9eda-8914c1072439/aenm202403306-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2d9838f7-cd86-456c-913e-0a1a33df0af7/aenm202403306-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403306-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403306" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM images of a) NFPP and e) HC electrodes after cycling. Depth distribution of B<sup>3+</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> ion fragment contents characterized by TOF-SIMS for b) NFPP and f) HC electrodes. Distribution of elements in SEI films on c,d) NFPP and g,h) HC electrodes analyzed by TOF-SIMS. XPS spectra of C 1, O 1, F 1, and B 1s surface of i) NFPP and j) HC electrodes after 50 cycles with NaDFOB-G2 electrolyte.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In summary, we have successfully developed a facile, cost-efficient, and eco-friendly method for the synthesis of sodium difluoro(oxalato)borate (NaDFOB). This compound is utilized as the solo electrolyte salt, demonstrating remarkable compatibility with various electrode materials. The NaF-rich inorganic SEI film derived from the reductive decomposition of NaDFOB has excellent interfacial compatibility to guarantee the stabilization of the electrolyte for achieving long-term cycling stability of the cells. As a result, the Ah-level HC//NFPP pouch cell, utilizing the NaDFOB-based ether electrolyte, achieves an average Coulombic efficiency exceeding 99.9% and maintains 80% of its initial capacity over 500 cycles. Additionally, it exhibits impressive cycling performance across a broad temperature range from −20 to 60&nbsp;°C. This study offers valuable insights and inspiration for the development of advanced sodium salts, potentially accelerating the practical adoption of sodium-ion batteries in diverse applications.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
